        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525"><meta property="og:title" content="DFT-Guided Design of Dual Dopants in Anatase TiO2 for Boosted Sodium Storage"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/acs.nanolett.4c03525.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Anatase titanium dioxide (TiO2) has drawn great attention as an anode material in sodium ion batteries (SIBs), but it suffers from the sluggish diffusion kinetics of Na+ within TiO2 and inferior electronic conductivity. Herein, under the guidance of density functional theory (DFT), we propose a nitrogen and selenium dual-doped TiO2 system as an advanced SIB anode. Both DFT and experimental investigations reveal the cooperative effect of dopants in boosting the electrochemical performance of TiO2, finding the optimal content ratio (N/Se at 1:3) for overall improved SIB performances. As expected, experimental results exhibit excellent sodium storage behavior of the N,Se-doped TiO2, including high discharge capacity (142 mAh g–1 at 2 A g–1), good rate performance (82 mAh g–1 at 20 A g–1), and ultralong cyclability (97% retention over 5000 cycles at 2 A g–1). Our study underscores the importance of dual-heteroatom doping in the rational design of advanced electrode materials."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/acs.nanolett.4c03525.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6e266aa5c775c-LHR"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e266aa5c775c-LHR">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'd7e87914-ee05-4ddb-86c4-7f29e06d6aa8'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e266aa5c775c-LHR">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "DFT-Guided Design of Dual Dopants in Anatase TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; for Boosted Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "DFT-Guided Design of Dual Dopants in Anatase TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; for Boosted Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "DFT-Guided Design of Dual Dopants in Anatase TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; for Boosted Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "DFT-Guided Design of Dual Dopants in Anatase TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; for Boosted Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "DFT-Guided Design of Dual Dopants in Anatase TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; for Boosted Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "DFT-Guided Design of Dual Dopants in Anatase TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; for Boosted Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "DFT-Guided Design of Dual Dopants in Anatase TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; for Boosted Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "DFT-Guided Design of Dual Dopants in Anatase TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; for Boosted Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "DFT-Guided Design of Dual Dopants in Anatase TiO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; for Boosted Sodium Storage", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

China</div></div><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Dongyuan++Zhao" data-id="article_author_search">Dongyuan Zhao</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" src="/products/achs/releasedAssets/images/orchid-2856f829046fbda55b90e1582edf0e9a.png" alt="Orcid"><a href="https://orcid.org/0000-0001-8440-6902" title="Orcid link">https://orcid.org/0000-0001-8440-6902</a></div></div></div></span></li><li><span><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor" tabindex="0" data-id="article_author_info" property="author">Kun Lan</span><strong>*</strong><div class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div class="loa-info--wrapper"><div class="loa-info-name">Kun Lan</div><div class="loa-info-affiliations"><div class="loa-info-affiliations-info">College of Energy Materials and Chemistry, College of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Inner Mongolia University, Hohhot 010021, P. R. China</div></div><span class="conrtib-corresp"><strong>*</strong>Email: <a href="mailto:k_lan@imu.edu.cn">k_lan@imu.edu.cn</a></span><span class="conrtib-corresp"></span><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Kun++Lan" data-id="article_author_search">Kun Lan</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" src="/products/achs/releasedAssets/images/orchid-2856f829046fbda55b90e1582edf0e9a.png" alt="Orcid"><a href="https://orcid.org/0000-0001-8983-0155" title="Orcid href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D14957%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DShuang%2BLi%252C%2BXu%2BWen%252C%2BXin%2BMiao%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D24%26issueNum%3D47%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.4c03525%26title%3DDFT-Guided%2BDesign%2Bof%2BDual%2BDopants%2Bin%2BAnatase%2BTiO2%2Bfor%2BBoosted%2BSodium%2BStorage%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D14964%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2024" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/medium/nl4c03525_0005.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/large/nl4c03525_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Anatase titanium dioxide (TiO<sub>2</sub>) has drawn great attention as an anode material in sodium ion batteries (SIBs), but it suffers from the sluggish diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> within TiO<sub>2</sub> and inferior electronic conductivity. Herein, under the guidance of density functional theory (DFT), we propose a nitrogen and selenium dual-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> system as an advanced SIB anode. Both DFT and experimental investigations reveal the cooperative effect of dopants in boosting the electrochemical performance of TiO<sub>2</sub>, finding the optimal content ratio (N/Se at 1:3) for overall improved SIB performances. As expected, experimental results exhibit excellent sodium storage behavior of the N,Se-doped TiO<sub>2</sub>, including high discharge capacity (142 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>), good rate performance (82 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>), and ultralong cyclability (97% retention over 5000 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>). Our study underscores the importance of dual-heteroatom doping in the rational design of advanced electrode materials.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D14957%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DShuang%2BLi%252C%2BXu%2BWen%252C%2BXin%2BMiao%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D24%26issueNum%3D47%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.4c03525%26title%3DDFT-Guided%2BDesign%2Bof%2BDual%2BDopants%2Bin%2BAnatase%2BTiO2%2Bfor%2BBoosted%2BSodium%2BStorage%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D14964%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Diffusion" class="article__tags__link">Diffusion</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290714&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Doping" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Doping" class="article__tags__link">Doping</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291999&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Minerals" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Minerals" class="article__tags__link">Minerals</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290689&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxides" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Oxides" class="article__tags__link">Oxides</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Theoretical+Simulation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_theoretical%20simulation">theoretical simulation</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=DFT&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_DFT">DFT</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Titanium+Dioxide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_titanium%20dioxide">titanium dioxide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Doping&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_doping">doping</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><p class="first">For decades, rechargeable batteries have been extensively applied in diverse fields, such as portable electronics, electric vehicles and devices, and power systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> Because of the abundant sodium resource and similar properties to lithium, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have gradually become a promising alternative in energy storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 4 to 10">(4−10)</a> However, one of huge limitations of SIBs is the deficiency of fast and reversible Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction because of its large ionic radius and high ionization potential. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11,12)</a> To overcome this challenge, tremendous efforts have been made in exploring suitable sodium host materials as desirable SIB electrodes, for example, metal oxides and carbon-based materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> Among them, because of the advantageous characteristics of remarkable chemical stability, high theoretical capacity (335 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), high abundance, and nontoxicity, anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> has been considered as an excellent anode for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 14 to 17">(14−17)</a> However, the sodium storage performance of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> is severely restricted by its low intrinsic electronic conductivity and the sluggish diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> within TiO<sub>2</sub> frameworks. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 18 to 20">(18−20)</a></p><p>To address this problem, doping TiO<sub>2</sub> with heteroatoms (e.g., N, P, S, F, and Se) has been extensively acknowledged as a useful strategy to increase its intrinsic electronic conductivity and substantially enhance sodium storage performance by introducing defects and oxygen vacancies (O<sub>v</sub>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 21 to 27">(21−27)</a> For instance, the introduction of N dopant into TiO<sub>2</sub> is an efficient strategy to increase the carrier density and modify the bandgap structure, resulting in improved electrical conductivity and battery performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Recent progress has also witnessed the deep-cation-site-rich S-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> with reduced bandgap and accelerated mobility rate of holes <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> and Se-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> for ameliorated electronic conductivity, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> leading to superior rate capability and cycling stability. Unfortunately, despite the massive endeavors on single-heteroatom doping with only one aspect of property enhancement, dual-heteroatom-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> models, which possess synergistic effects in boosting overall electrochemical performance, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> still remain rarely explored. The systematic understanding of the effects of complicated doping systems on sodium storage performance is of great importance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Nevertheless, the experimental selection of dopants that have compatible and positive effects with optimal performance improvement is the key challenge. Density functional theory (DFT) has emerged as a valuable and cost-effective alternative to experiments which enables evaluation of material properties and provides guidance for electrode design. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 33 to 39">(33−39)</a> A few examples include the DFT calculations in predicting the properties of N, S-codoped TiO<sub>2</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> and N, F-codoping <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> for increasing electrochemical performance. Therefore, the rational theoretical prediction and experimental design of dual-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> materials would enable access to advanced model platforms for high-performance sodium storage, which has rarely been explored so far.</p><p>In this work, we selected a dual-doped anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> material model by incorporating N<sup>3–</sup> and Se<sup>4+</sup> ions as codopants based on DFT prediction for boosting SIB performance. Specifically, we applied DFT to comparatively investigate the bandgap (Δ<i>E</i><sub>gap</sub>) and the energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion (Δ<i>E</i><sub>bar</sub>) of a single heteroatom and dual heteroatoms of N and Se in anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> to evaluate the sodium storage behavior. It is revealed that the Se atom replaces Ti and N replaces O, which can realize high electronic conductivity and accelerated Na<sup>+</sup> transfer kinetics within TiO<sub>2</sub>, and the optimal N/Se content ratio is about 1:3. Guided by the DFT calculation, the N, Se-codoped anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles (denoted as N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3) were synthesized through a simple one-pot solution-processed method. During the doping process, the mutual promoted diffusion and mobility for N and Se elements within TiO<sub>2</sub> renders a high doping concentration. Experimental data further illustrate that the N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 possesses a dopant ratio of 0.96% for N and 3.17% for Se within anatase lattices. Because of the cooperative advantages of dual dopants, the N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3, as an anode material, exhibits much enhanced sodium storage performance with a much improved capacity of 142 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a high rate capability of 103 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and long cycling stability (97% over 5000 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> and 88% retention over 10,000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>). The improved diffusion coefficient, together with the overwhelming capacitive contribution of 94% in dynamic analysis, further validates the synergistic effect of dual dopants in regulating electronic transfer and ionic diffusion kinetics.</p><p>As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>A, DFT calculations were conducted to guide the experimental design of the dual-doped anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> anode. To gain a deep insight into the doping effect on the electronic structure of TiO<sub>2</sub>, the bandgaps (Δ<i>E</i><sub>gap</sub>) between the valence band (VB) and conduction band (CB) were calculated. It was found that the bandgap value of pure anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> is 2.0 eV (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>B), while the introduction of a single N (0.93 atom %) or Se (0.93 atom %) atom can narrow the bandgap to 1.6 and 1.7 eV, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>C,D). A remarkable decrease to 0.6 eV for the N, Se dual-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> further implies a substantial increase in conductivity though heteroatom doping (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>E). Moreover, the energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion (Δ<i>E</i><sub>bar</sub>), which determine the charge transfer kinetics, were calculated (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>F), and the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion pathways are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. For the single N-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> (0.93 atom %), a decreased Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier of 0.47 eV compared to pristine anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (0.53 eV) can be realized. The lower Δ<i>E</i><sub>bar</sub> of 0.37 eV for Se-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> (0.93 atom %) suggests the easier Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion capability within anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>.</p><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/medium/nl4c03525_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/large/nl4c03525_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. DFT-guided theoretical calculations of dual-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> models. (A) Scheme of DFT-guided design of dopants in TiO<sub>2</sub> material for SIBs. (B–E) Densities of states (DOS) of (B) anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>, (C) N (0.93 atom %)-TiO<sub>2</sub>, (D) Se (0.93 atom %)-TiO<sub>2</sub>, and (E) N (0.93 atom %), Se (2.79 atom %)-TiO<sub>2</sub>, respectively. (F) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers of the pure, single-doped, and dual-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> models. (G, H) Charge densities from front and side views of the N (0.93 atom %), Se (2.79 atom %)-TiO<sub>2</sub>. The yellow color indicates electron depletion, and the blue color indicates electron accumulation.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/large/nl4c03525_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>Based on the above results, it is evident that N doping serves to significantly reduce the bandgap for remarkably improved electronic conductivity, while the Se doping is conducive to reducing the energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion to promote Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. In this regard, we selected the N, Se-codoped TiO<sub>2</sub> as the model material and further investigated the influence of doping content by DFT through calculating various N and Se atom ratios in the 108-atom supercell (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). Among them, the N (0.93 atom %), Se (2.79 atom %)-TiO<sub>2</sub> model shows the minimum Δ<i>E</i><sub>gap</sub> of 0.6 eV and Δ<i>E</i><sub>bar</sub> of 0.29 eV (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S4 and S5 and Table S1</a>), suggesting the N/Se ratio of 1:3 is most conducive to the improvement of conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. In addition, compared to the density of states (DOS) of N-TiO<sub>2</sub> and Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>, the introduction of Se produces a new state and exhibits more occupations around the Fermi level in the presence of N atom (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>), which further advances the electronic conductivity of TiO<sub>2</sub>. The diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> is facilitated within the N (0.93 atom %), Se (2.79 atom %)-TiO<sub>2</sub> mode attributable to the imbalanced distribution of charge around doping sites engendered by the formation of a built-in electric field (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>G,H).</p><p>Guided by the theoretical prediction, anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>, N-TiO<sub>2</sub>, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>, and N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub> samples with varied N/Se ratios were synthesized by the one-pot solution-processed method with SeCl<sub>4</sub> as the Se source and urea as the N source, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>A. The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image displays the spherical morphology with average particle size of about 200 nm for the pure TiO<sub>2</sub> samples, while the particle sizes are slightly enlarged after N or Se doping (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). For the N, Se dual-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> samples, minor agglomeration of nanospheres can be observed, possibly attributed to the formation of defects and dislocations during heteroatom doping. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> From the high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images, the doped TiO<sub>2</sub> samples possess lattice spacings slightly less than 0.352 nm, associated with the (101) plane of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). The decrease in interplanar distance is ascribed to the smaller ionic radius of substituted Se<sup>4+</sup> (64.0 pm) compared with Ti<sup>4+</sup> (74.5 pm). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> The elemental mapping images exhibit the uniform distributions of Ti, O, N, and Se for these single- and dual-doped anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> samples (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>), confirming the successful doping of N and Se. X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of all TiO<sub>2</sub> samples show a typical anatase phase (JCPDS no. 21-1272) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>B and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S10A</a>), indicating the constant crystal structure before and after doping. Notably, compared with the anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>, a slight peak shift to 25.4° of these doped TiO<sub>2</sub> samples can be observed due to narrowed interplanar spacing, which also implies the existence of dopants in the lattice. In addition, the selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern shows clear and well-resolved diffraction rings (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>C), suggesting a polycrystallized anatase structure.</p><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/medium/nl4c03525_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/large/nl4c03525_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Synthesis and characterizations of the designed dual-doped TiO<sub>2</sub>. (A) Schematic illustration of the formation process for the N, Se-codoped TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles. (B) XRD patterns. (C) SAED pattern of N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3. (D) EPR spectra of all the nanoparticles. (E, F) High-resolution Ti 2p and O 1s XPS spectra for the TiO<sub>2</sub>, N-TiO<sub>2</sub>, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>, and N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 samples. (G, H) High-resolution N 1s and Se 3d XPS spectra for N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3. (I) Se contents in the Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>, N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/1, N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/2, and N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 samples from ICP-OES analysis.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/large/nl4c03525_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>To further investigate the dopants in samples, electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) measurements were conducted (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>D and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S10B</a>). The signals at a <i>g</i> value of 2.003 are observed for undoped or doped TiO<sub>2</sub>, which originates from unpaired electrons trapped by oxygen vacancies. Besides, the peak intensities revealed that N doping and N, Se codoping favored the formation of more O<sub>v</sub>. In Ti 2p X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analyses (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>E), two peaks at 464.8 eV (Ti 2p<sub>1/2</sub>) and 459.1 eV (Ti 2p<sub>2/3</sub>) for N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub> are assigned to the Ti<sup>4+</sup> oxidation state. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> The O 1s XPS spectra show two chemical states, and the sharp peaks at 530.1 and 531.1 eV are assigned to the O–Ti and O–H bonds (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>F). Note that the positive peak shift of Ti 2p and O 1s after N doping implies the existence of vacancies due to electron transfer from the N and Se additives. Furthermore, in the N 1s XPS spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>G), the peaks located at 397.9 and 399.2 eV can be attributed to the N–Se and O–Ti–N bonds, indicating the partial substitution of N to lattice oxygen. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The Se 3d XPS spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>H) of N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 exhibits two peaks located at 58.5 and 61.8 eV, assigned to the Se<sup>4+</sup> and Ti 3s species, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> respectively, which also supports the substitution of Ti sites by Se dopant.</p><p>The N and Se contents in TiO<sub>2</sub> were further investigated. From XPS analyses, the N contents within N-TiO<sub>2</sub>, N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/1, N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/2, and N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 were 0.89, 0.85, 0.56, and 0.96 atom %, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2 and Figure S11</a>). Meanwhile, the Se contents within Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>, N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/1, N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/2, and N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 were 0.96, 0.64, 1.15, and 3.17 atom %, respectively. With the increase of SeCl<sub>4</sub> amount from 0.05 to 0.15 g in the synthesis, the Se content in the N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub> sample gradually increased from 0.64 to 3.17 atom %, in good accordance with the energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) and ICP-OES results (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>I and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S12</a>). The Se content in N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/1 (0.64 atom %) is lower than that in Se-TiO<sub>2</sub> (0.96 atom %), possibly because the excessive N hinder the Se<sup>4+</sup> diffusion and mobility within anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>.</p><p>To investigate the doping effect, the electrochemical properties of N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub> as a SIB anode were investigated compared to pure and single-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> samples. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>A, the N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 electrode exhibits the highest initial discharge/charge capacity of 287/247 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The corresponding initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) is calculated to be as high as 86.1%, which is much higher than those of the TiO<sub>2</sub>, N-TiO<sub>2</sub>, and Se-TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode, suggesting a significant improvement after N, Se codoping within anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>. In the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) curves for these four electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>B and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S13A</a>), the N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 electrode shows the smallest diffusion resistance of 14.5 Ω, confirming the enhanced electronic conductivity. Furthermore, in situ EIS analyses were conducted. The delayed Nyquist plots of the N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 sample reveal a small semicircle at high frequencies and a sharp line at low frequencies, showing the capacitive behavior (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13B</a>). In addition, the voltage drop of 0.682 V for N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 is much smaller than that of 1.305 V for anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>C), which also validates the increased conductivity induced by N, Se codoping.</p><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/medium/nl4c03525_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/large/nl4c03525_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical measurements of the doped TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes. (A) Charge–discharge profiles of the first cycle at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the TiO<sub>2</sub>, N-TiO<sub>2</sub>, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>, and N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 samples. (B) EIS curves of the TiO<sub>2</sub>, N-TiO<sub>2</sub>, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>, and N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 samples. (C) Galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of the TiO<sub>2</sub> and N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 electrodes at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (D) Galvanostatic charge–discharge curves at current densities from 1 to 50 A g<sup>–1</sup> for the N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 electrode. (E) Rate performances of the TiO<sub>2</sub>, N-TiO<sub>2</sub>, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>, and N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 electrodes ramping from 0.1 to 20 A g<sup>–1</sup> and back to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (F) Cycling stabilities of the four electrodes during 5000 cycles at 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (G) Cycling stabilities of the TiO<sub>2</sub> and N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 electrodes over 10,000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (H) Comparison of electrochemical properties of these four electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/large/nl4c03525_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The galvanostatic charge–discharge curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>D) at current densities varying from 1 to 50 A g<sup>–1</sup> indicate the ultrahigh rate capability of the N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 sample, which can be attributed to the ultrafast pseudocapacitive feature of this TiO<sub>2</sub> material. The rate performance of the N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 was further measured and compared with other contrast samples (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>E). It can be found from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>E that the N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 electrode exhibits higher and stabler capacities than the other three electrodes at each current. More interestingly, the rate performances and EIS profiles of N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/1, N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/2, and N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 were also measured to further clarify the influence of TiO<sub>2</sub> modified with different molar ratios of N/Se. All three of these dual-doped samples possess reversible capacities from 0.1 to 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 delivers the highest rate capacities and the lowest resistance among the three electrodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13C,D</a>). This response implies that more Se dopant could facilitate diffusion kinetics within TiO<sub>2</sub> for a larger sodium storage capability.</p><p>From long-term cycling measurements at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>F and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S13E</a>), all these TiO<sub>2</sub>-based electrodes display good durability for over 5000 cycles with retentions over 85% (after precycling at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for 50 cycles). Among them, N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 delivers the highest and most stable reversible capacity of 142 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over cycles, demonstrating its excellent long-term durability. After 5000 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the nanostructure and crystalline phase of the N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 electrode are well-maintained, which proves that the electrode materials have a stable structure (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>). Moreover, when the current obviously increases to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 can still show a reversible capacity of 104 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a retention of 88% even after 10,000 cycles, which are much higher than those of the TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>G). All of these electrochemical performances of N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 are outstanding in comparison with other TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>H).</p><p>To gain further insight into the sodium storage electrochemistry, kinetic analyses were carried out. First, the galvanostatic intermittent titration (GITT) technique was used to obtain the diffusion coefficient of sodium ions based on the charging process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>A,B). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>A,B, the N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 electrode exhibits the promoted Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient <i>D</i>(Na<sup>+</sup>) during the sodium storage process (from 3.3 × 10<sup>–10</sup> to 4.0 × 10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>), which is much higher than those for single-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> and anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>B). The highest <i>D</i>(Na<sup>+</sup>) in the whole charge process manifests the modified electrochemical dynamics of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> after doping, which is in accordance with the DFT calculations. Besides, cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves were measured at different scan rates from 0.2 to 10 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>C), displaying gradually increased peak currents and similar broad peaks with increasing currents. The <i>b</i> values of the cathodic and anodic peaks were fitted to be 0.89 and 0.92, respectively; these values below 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> imply the dominated pseudocapacitive process within the N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>D). Based on the quantification from capacitive- and insertion-type capacity, the proportion of the pseudocapacitive contribution increases from 0.2 to 10 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>E), demonstrating the enhanced capacitive behavior. When the scan rate reaches to 10 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, a dominant proportion of 94% of the total charge/discharge contribution is derived from pseudocapacitive process for N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3, while the anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> shows a much lower capacitive contribution (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>F and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S15</a>), indicating that more ions are capable of fast diffusion into the doped TiO<sub>2</sub> material.</p><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/medium/nl4c03525_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/large/nl4c03525_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Dynamic analyses of the dual-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> electrode. (A) GITT profiles. (B) Diffusion coefficients calculated from GITT profiles as a function of potential for TiO<sub>2</sub>, N-TiO<sub>2</sub>, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>, and N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3. (C) CV curves at various scan rates from 0.2 to 10 mV s<sup>–1</sup> for N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3. (D) Analysis of <i>b</i> values using the relationship between peak current and scan rate. (E) Contribution ratio of the capacitive- and diffusion-controlled charge versus scan rate. (F) Separation of the capacitive and diffusion currents in N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 at a scan rate of 10 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (G) Schematic illustration of the dopant engineering in TiO<sub>2</sub> material for fast pseudocapacitive charge storage.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/asset/images/large/nl4c03525_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>Our results confirm the cooperative doping effect of N and Se dopants within TiO<sub>2</sub> in electrochemical features of N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub> as a SIB anode. The improved electrochemical sodium storage properties are attributed to the synergistic effect of dual dopants in the TiO<sub>2</sub> lattice (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>G). The N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 sample featuring a high Se concentration and a low N concentration provides abundant active sites for Na ion storage, endowing it with an excellent reversible charge storage capability. The N doping allows for effectively narrowing the bandgap of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>, while the Se doping enables boosting of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion within TiO<sub>2</sub> lattice, thereby giving rise to remarkably enhanced electronic conductivity as well as accelerated sodium ion transfer kinetics. Moreover, these defect sites were conducive to ultrafast Na ion storage without causing a huge volume expansion, thereby leading to a high rate capability and cycling stability.</p><p class="last">In summary, we present a type of N, Se dual-heteroatom-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> material model to achieve boosted sodium storage performance from both theoretical calculation and experimental investigation. The N, Se-codoped anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles with regulated dopant ratio were synthesized through a simple one-pot solution-processed method. DFT calculations reveal that the N dopant enables efficient narrowing of the bandgap of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> while the Se dopant favors acceleration of the sodium ion transfer kinetics, and an optimal N/Se ratio of 1/3 leads to the minimum bandgap (Δ<i>E</i><sub>gap</sub> = 0.6 eV) and energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion (Δ<i>E</i><sub>bar</sub> = 0.29 eV). As predicted, the constructed N, Se dual-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> with atomic ratios of 0.96 atom % for N and 3.17 atom % for Se exhibits boosted electrochemical properties in comparison with anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles due to the mutual promoted diffusion and mobility within anatase lattices. As a SIB anode, the obtained N, Se-TiO<sub>2</sub>-1/3 sample shows an improved capacity of 142 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, good rate capability (103 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>), and long cyclability (over 5000 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> and 10,000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>). Moreover, dynamic analyses show the greatly promoted diffusion coefficient and dominant capacitive contribution of 94% at 10 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, which further confirm the synergistic effect of dual dopants in facilitating the charge transfer capability in TiO<sub>2</sub>. Our study underlines the significance of constructing incorporated heteroatom dopants for high-performance sodium storage electrodes and paves a combined pathway for predicting and designing advanced nanomaterials.</p><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Additional experimental details for the calculations, characterizations of materials, and electrochemical measurements (Figures S1–S15 and Tables S1–S3) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525/suppl_file/nl4c03525_si_001.pdf">nl4c03525_si_001.pdf (2.08 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.4c03525" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Defect-assisted selective surface phosphorus doping to enhance rate capability of titanium dioxide for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gan, Qingmeng; He, Hanna; Zhu, Youhuan; Wang, Zhenyu; Qin, Ning; Gu, Shuai; Li, Zhiqiang; Luo, Wen; Lu, Zhouguang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9247-9258</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Phosphorus doping is an effective strategy to simultaneously improve the electronic cond. and regulate the ionic diffusion kinetics of TiO2 being considered as anode materials for sodium ion batteries.  However, efficient phosphorus doping at high concn. in well-crystd. TiO2 nanoparticles is still a big challenge.  Herein, the authors propose a defect-assisted phosphorus doping strategy to selectively engineer the surface structure of TiO2 nanoparticles.  The reduced TiO2-x shell layer that is rich in oxygen defects and Ti3+ species precisely triggered a high concn. of phosphorus doping (∼7.8 at.%), and consequently a TiO2@TiO2-x-P core@shell architecture was produced.  Comprehensive characterizations and first-principle calcns. proved that the surface-functionalized TiO2-x-P thin layer endowed the TiO2@TiO2-x-P with substantially enhanced electronic cond. and accelerated Na ion transportation, resulting in great rate capability (167 mA h g-1 at 10 000 mA g-1) and stable cycling (99% after 5000 cycles at 10 A g-1).  Combining in/ex situ x-ray diffraction with ex situ ESR clearly demonstrated the high reversibility and robust mech. behavior of TiO2@TiO2-x-P upon long-term cycling.  This work provides an interesting and effective strategy for precise heteroatoms doping to improve the electrochem. performance of nanoparticles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoV2X3aWTMl9bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhHWhCDrNXjdA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhsVamtb7P&amp;md5=cd8e232f3259e7881601dbcf23b2e6ee</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2022.03.043"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, Y.                 10.1016/j.ensm.2021.06.033</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D41%26publication_year%3D2021%26pages%3D522-545%26journal%3DEnergy%2BStorage%2BMater.%26author%3DL.%2BP.%2BZhang%26author%3DX.%2BL.%2BLi%26author%3DM.%2BR.%2BYang%26author%3DW.%2BH.%2BChen%26title%3DHigh-safety%2Bseparators%2Bfor%2Blithium-ion%2Bbatteries%2Band%2Bsodium-ion%2Bbatteries%253A%2BAdvances%2Band%2Bperspective%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.ensm.2021.06.033&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.4c03525&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.ensm.2021.06.033&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J. Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S. T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y. K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-ion batteries: Present and future</span>. <i>Chem. Soc. Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>46</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3529</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3614</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C6CS00776G</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D46%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D3529-3614%26journal%3DChem.%2BSoc.%2BRev.%26author%3DJ.%2BY.%2BHwang%26author%3DS.%2BT.%2BMyung%26author%3DY.%2BK.%2BSun%26title%3DSodium-ion%2Bbatteries%253A%2BPresent%2Band%2Bfuture%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC6CS00776G&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.4c03525&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC6CS00776G&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh0DRGwdoOd-g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202304251"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, H.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Understanding of the sodium storage mechanism in hard carbon anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Xiaoyang; Liu, Changyu; Fang, Yongjin; Ai, Xinping; Zhong, Faping; Yang, Hanxi; Cao, Yuliang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1133-1150</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CEANFS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2637-9368</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">John Wiley &amp; Sons Australia, Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Hard carbon has been regarded as the most promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its low cost, high reversible capacity, and low working potential.  However, the uncertain sodium storage mechanism hinders the rational design and synthesis of high-performance hard carbon anode materials for practical SIBs.  During the past decades, tremendous efforts have been put to stimulate the development of hard carbon materials.  In this review, we discuss the recent progress of the study on the sodium storage mechanism of hard carbon anodes, and the effective strategies to improve their sodium storage performance have been summarized.  It is anticipated that hard carbon anodes with high electrochem. properties will be inspired and fabricated for large-scale energy storage applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrJBzNU_bMKCrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh0DRGwdoOd-g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB38XhsVGgsL%252FJ&amp;md5=7990adfff3d72f282da700e66ef36276</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1039/D3EE01154B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, X.
